Claims arising from any other business pursuit or operation are insured only if liability coverage for it is shown on the coverage summary page.

8.7.8 Exclusions of Coverage

As we know, exclusions are the norm, and Section II: Liability Coverages is no exception. In addition to the list of exclusions, there is no coverage for claims arising from:

• War, invasion, or act of a foreign enemy.

• Business pursuits or any business use of the premises except as provided under the Business and Business Property section.

• The rendering or failure to render any professional service. The law puts a higher standard of duty on those having specialized skill or knowledge, and persons providing such service should purchase professional liability insurance.

• Bodily injury or property damage caused by any intentional or criminal act or failure to act by:

- Any person insured by this policy, or

- Any other person at the direction of any person insured by this policy.

• Sexual, physical, psychological, or emotional abuse from molestation or harassment, including corporal punishment by, at the direction of, or with the knowledge, of any person insured by this policy, or

- Failure of any person insured by this policy to take steps to prevent sexual, physical, psychological, or emotional abuse, molestation, harassment, or corporal punishment.

• The transmission of communicable disease by any person insured by the policy.

• The ownership, use, or operation of:

- Any aircraft.

- Premises used as an airport or landing facility and all activities related to either.

Note: Insurers have different property policies and, as such, may have different exclusions concerning the legal cultivation of cannabis. It is important to check the individual wordings for each company.

8.8 PERSONAL UMBRELLA POLICIES

“How much is too much?” is a question often asked when dealing with a product such as liability insurance. Though many companies have a minimum limit to be applied to all policies, the upper limit is variable and depends on the exposures presented by the insured and the appetite of the company to provide a higher limit. A thorough analysis of an insured will show areas where a higher limit is required or where extra coverage is required because of an exclusion on the primary policy. For the insured, the risks may be identified and increased limits may be required, but the primary insurer may be unable to provide the limits and coverage that is required by the insured.

Image

Every insurer has limits on the amount of insurance it can write on a primary policy, be it property or automobile. They may have specific limits noted in their underwriting to control their exposures, or they may not have reinsurance in place to take on increased limits. This leaves the insured in a situation where they may have to purchase a separate policy to give themselves the increased protection they require. They can either purchase an excess policy or an umbrella policy.

An excess policy will provide the same coverages and exclusions as the primary policy and will only pay when the primary policy limits are exhausted. Where extra coverage is required because of an exclusion in the primary the insured can buy an umbrella policy.

To be eligible for an umbrella liability policy, insureds must provide the insurer with evidence that they already have existing or primary liability policies in place for any known exposures, such as automobile, watercraft, aviation, or residences.

When there is a claim, the umbrella liability policy will pay only when:

• There is not enough insurance under the primary policy. If the primary policy has a limit of $2 million, this limit must be exhausted before the umbrella policy pays in excess of this limit.

• There is no coverage provided under the primary policy. In this scenario, the umbrella policy will pay as the primary policy, provided it does not contain the same exclusions as the primary policy. This is called the “drop down” feature. In this case, there is a self-insured retention (SIR) amount (which varies from company to company) if this umbrella becomes the primary insurer. This SIR functions as a deductible.

8.9 OTHER LIABILITY POLICIES

8.9.1 Commercial Liability

The purpose of this section is to make you aware of the overall nature of commercial liability and to help you understand that it is much more complex in its coverages and application than the personal liability coverage we just described. We’ll cover a general overview of a commercial liability policy and some of its features. However, you’ll need to study beyond this textbook to serve the needs of a commercial client.

Just as a habitational policy is issued with personal liability included, so a commercial property policy includes commercial liability. While all habitational policies comprise the same policy coverages, commercial liability is underwritten based on the activities and risk associated with each commercial risk. This means the one-size-fits-all approach does not apply to commercial insurance. The insuring agreement is the same, and the supplemental payments are the same, but the activities of the business will determine the coverages, rates, and limits.

These coverages and rates can be affected by events far from the location of the individual insured. Disasters and catastrophes around the world have an influence on reinsurance rates, which directly affect individual rates and availability of coverage.

The form used in Canada is the Commercial General Liability (CGL) Policy (IBC 2100). This policy is 22 pages in length, and it is quite detailed and extensive. It is broad enough in scope to be able to respond to the liability exposures of individuals, corporations, general or limited partnerships, societies (such as clubs or religious groups), and government bodies. However, as with all policies, there are exclusions and the broker should do a thorough analysis of the insured to be aware of these exclusions and bring them to the attention of the insured.

Similar to habitations, businesses also have the general liability exposures based on the condition of their premises and their operations or activities. Whether the business is a retail operation (such as a hardware store catering to the public) or a manufacturing operation with no public access, there is a liability exposure. An appliance store has both on- and off-premises exposure in the showroom and the installation of appliances in customers’ homes. There is an exposure for medical payments if a visitor injures themselves on-site, just like Section F: Voluntary Medical Payments in a Homeowner Policy. A tenant in a building has a tenant’s legal liability exposure based on its care, custody, and control of the space it leases and occupies.

However, businesses do have some unique exposures that habitational policies don’t:

• Tenant’s legal liability: When a business owns a building, damage to the building would come under the building section of their property policy. When a business rents or leases a property, they have an exposure in tort law for damage to the property in their care, custody, and control. Section A of the liability policy excludes payment for damages to premises they occupy. This gap in coverage is provided by tenant’s legal liability insurance. The coverage is limited to payment for only those losses for which the tenant is legally liable in tort (negligent); liabilities assumed under contract are excluded but can be insured under a separate policy. The amount purchased should be no less than the value of the building or portion of the building occupied.

• Product liability: Generally, a product is anything sold by the insured. Consumers have the right to expect that the goods they purchase will be safe. When their use results in injury or damage, consumers have a right to take legal action against the party that made or sold the defective product. Business liability policies provide coverage for this exposure.

• Completed operations liability: When completed work is put to its intended use and injury or damage results, the party responsible for the work may be sued for damages. Appliance repair shops and plumbing, heating, and electrical contractors all have a completed operations liability exposure.

• Personal and advertising injury liability: Personal and advertising injury means injury, including consequential bodily injury, arising out of one or more of the following offences:

- Libel and slander.

- Malicious persecution.

- Wrongful entry or eviction.

- Invasion of privacy.

- False arrest.

- Detention or imprisonment.

- Unauthorized use of another’s advertising idea.

In an action alleging personal injury, the wronged party may seek damage for extreme humiliation, stress, or other personal injury.

• Hold harmless agreement: Also known as a hold harmless clause or hold harmless provision, this is a clause in a legal contract that absolves one party of legal liability for any injuries or damages suffered by another party. It plays a crucial role in a business transaction where the risk of injury is high. It is essentially a risk transfer mechanism and an indication of the extent of risk each party is prepared to assume. Careful drafting of the clause determines the extent of the protection the clause will provide the recipient of the hold harmless agreement.

For example, a homeowner having their roof repaired would have the roofer sign a hold harmless agreement to absolve the homeowners from liability if the roofer were to fall off the roof. A hold harmless clause is also printed on the back of a ski lift ticket absolving the ski operator from liability if the skier were to be injured on the hill. Commercially, a tenant may have to sign a hold harmless agreement absolving the landlord from any liability for damage if caused by the landlord. By signing or accepting the form, the signer has acknowledged the risks and agreed to accept them.

The hold harmless agreement does not always protect the person or company against a lawsuit or liability. The agreement must specifically state that the signer is waiving their rights to sue for negligence. Such agreements can be disregarded if they are vague or overly broad in language or believed to be fraudulent.

The CGL Policy also provides:

• Blanket contractual coverage for liability assumed by the insured, such as lease of premises, a side-track agreement, and other agreements.

• Contingent employer’s liability, which protects the insured for bodily injury to employees on whose behalf contributions are made by, or required to be made by, the insured under the provisions of any workers’ compensation law. If the workers’ compensation plan does not respond to a claim, the employer’s liability would respond.

• Owner’s and contractor’s protective liability, which provides the insured with coverage arising from the activities of a general contractor or sub-contractors.

These policies can also be extended by endorsement to include:

• Garage liability, which responds to the exclusion under the CGL Policy for payment for injury or damage to others arising out of the ownership, use, or operation of any automobile. This covers third-party liability for owned and non-owned vehicles and legal liability arising out of damage to customers’ automobiles while under their care, custody, and control.

• Limited environmental liability, which provides coverage for the sudden and accidental discharge, dispersal, release, or escape of pollutants. This coverage is only available on a selective basis.

• Employer’s bodily injury liability, which covers the insured with respect to claims of bodily injury to an employee not covered by workers’ compensation and arising out of and in the course of employment.

• It is important that both the broker and the insured understand the extent to which coverage is limited by Section III of the policy. Payment is based on both the Aggregate and Occurrence limit. In Personal lines there is no aggregate limit applied to the policy.

The aggregate limit stated on the policy is the most the policy will pay during the policy period for all claims for which insurance is provide28.

For example, if the policy contains a $2M aggregate limit, the insurer is not responsible for damages awarded more than that amount, whether by way of a single action or several actions, within the policy period. The policy may also state that there is a maximum payable per occurrence, say of $1M, with an aggregate of $2M. During the policy term, there are three losses; one for $1M, a second for $1.5M, and a third for $1.5M.

The first loss would be paid to the limit. The second loss would be capped at $1M, due to occurrence limit on the policy, therefore the insured would be responsible for $500,000. This second loss also breaches the $2M aggregate limit, therefore any subsequent claims, such as the third loss, would totally be the responsibility of the insured to pay.

Each of the many and varied policy wordings have definitions and exclusions particular to the them. It is important to know your client, know the company, and know the wordings!

8.9.2 Professional Liability

Although the CGL Policy is broad, it is not designed to insure the special liability needs of professionals — that’s where professional liability insurance comes in. The focus of professional liability policies is to provide payment for damages arising out of the rendering or failure to render professional services.

A professional is someone who possesses or claims to possess the special knowledge and skill necessary to render a professional service. When that knowledge or skill is deficient and financial or other injury results, a claim for damages may be initiated against the professional.

There are two major classes of professionals, including:

1) Those who administer health care, such as doctors, dentists, and pharmacists. Malpractice insurance will cover their legal liability arising out of the rendering or failure to render their professional service.

2) Those who give advice and counselling, such as lawyers, accountants, architects, and insurance and real estate brokers. Professionals having this exposure can buy errors and omissions (E&O) insurance to cover their exposure.
Occurrence-Based Policy Form 	Claims Made Policy form
The insurance company that was on the risk at the time the loss occurred will respond to the claim. 	The insurance company that is on the risk at the time the claim is made will respond.

These policies are written on a Claims Made Policy Form (IBC 2200), which is different from an Occurrence-based Policy Form (IBC 2100). The key difference between these two policies is that, under an Occurrence-based Policy, the insurance company that was on the risk at the time the loss occurred will respond to the claim. With a Claims Made Policy, the insurance company that is on the risk at the time the claim is made will respond. For many types of losses, the occurrence date may be indeterminate, but the date of the claim can be determined. For example, the exposure to asbestos may have occurred over a long period of time, making it hard to determine a fixed date of exposure; but the date of its manifestation as a cancer can be determined. These forms are highly detailed, and you’ll have to study them closely to be fully aware of the difference.

8.9.3 Commercial Umbrella Liability

In some cases, the liability policies purchased by the business may not address their complete liability insurance needs. Potential problems include:

• Inadequate limits because the primary insurer denied increased limits due to its own restrictions regarding reinsurance facilities and capacity.

• Inadequate coverage.

In these situations, providing an umbrella liability policy, as well as additional limits and broader policy coverages, can solve these problems. The umbrella policy is designed to respond only after the underlying or primary policy has responded. In the event of inadequate coverage under the primary policy, the insured must pay a deductible or self-insured retention (SIR) for additional coverage. This encourages the insured to buy adequate insurance for obvious liability exposure on the primary policy.

The application for umbrella liability coverage is quite extensive due to the nature of the policy. Furthermore, coverage cannot be bound by the broker — the policy comes into effect only after acceptance by the insurer. It’s also important to note that understanding the exclusions under an umbrella policy is just as important as understanding the exclusions under a primary policy.

8.10 CONCLUSION

This concludes our look at liability insurance, which is a mix of both legal history and specific policy wordings from the Homeowner Policy, Section II: Liability Coverages. You might be surprised at the broad range of risks that insureds can be liable for! This is a key area of insurance that will take time to fully comprehend but also to effectively apply. We recommend working with a mentor and asking questions of both your principal broker, your clients, and your underwriters to help you properly protect your clients and what they do.

GLOSSARY

Note: These terms are not statutory definitions and are not to be used for legislative interpretation or litigation purposes.

• Absolute liability: A category of liability applied to very dangerous or hazardous actions where negligence does not have to be proven. (Also known as Strict liability)

• Aggregate limit: The most the policy will pay during the policy period for all claims for which insurance is provided.

• Bailee: In the law of contracts and property, one to whom goods or property are entrusted. Can be either gratuitous (for no consideration) or for hire (for consideration).

• Concurrent causation: The simultaneous occurrence of two damaging events, one covered by insurance and the other not.

• Damages: The amount of money awarded to one person by another’s tort. Damages are injuries translated into dollars.

• Excess policy: A policy that provides that the insurer is liable only for the excess above and beyond that which may be collected on other insurance.

• Hold harmless agreement: A contractual arrangement whereby one party assumes legal responsibility for the acts of another.

• Liability insurance: Covers against third-party claims for bodily injury and property damage for which the insured is legally liable.

• Limit of liability: Maximum amount stated in a policy which an insurer is obligated to pay in the event of a loss.

• Negligence: Failure to use that degree of care which an ordinary person of reasonable prudence would use under the given circumstances.

• Occurrence: Generally, an unexpected event. In commercial liability policies, occurrence is usually defined as an accident, including continuous or repeated exposure to harmful conditions.

• Proximate cause: The most direct cause of loss; that is, the most effective — though not necessarily the last — in a series of events.

• Tenants legal liability: Insurance for loss or damage of a property resulting from an action of a person renting space at that property.

• Tort: A legal wrong other than a breach of contract, for which the court will provide a remedy in the form of a suit for damages.

• Tort-feasor: One who commits a tort, a wrong doer. “Joint tort-feasors” refers to two or more persons jointly or severally liable in tort for the same injury to a person or property.

REVIEW QUIZ

1) To prove any tort, the following three elements must be present: Duty owed, duty breached, and:

a) Loss of property.

b) Damages suffered as a result of the defendant’s direct action.

c) Potential for hazard or loss.

d) A peril insured against occurring.

2) Under Section II: Liability Coverages, the insurer does not agree to:

a) Investigate, negotiate, and settle claims.

b) Reimburse the insured for physiotherapy and other medical costs.

c) Defend civil actions against the insured.

d) Reimburse the insured for related out-of-pocket expenses.

3) The general purpose of Section E: Personal Liability is to give the insured protection for:

a) Injuries caused to others when the insured is negligent and held legally liable.

b) Careless use of other people’s property which the insured borrows.

c) Claims for injuries done to others, whether or not the insured is negligent.

d) Negligently caused damage to the insured’s own property.

4) Negligence is:

a) The failure to exercise the care that a reasonable person would take in a similar set of circumstances.

b) Behaving in a manner expected of a reasonable and prudent person.

c) Payment of insurance monies to a person injured by the insured persons.

d) Exercising rights under an insurance policy to recover money paid to a third person.

5) Negligence cannot be present if the person involved did not know that their conduct or action involved:

a) A peril.

b) An unreasonable risk of harm.

c) A dog.

d) A loss or damage.

6) In a liability situation, the activity that immediately precede the occurrence is called?

a) Preceding cause.

b) Proximate cause.

c) Presumptive cause.

d) Prefacing cause.

7) Which statement about liability insurance is not true?

a) Liability insurance is designed to pay only when the insured is legally liable.

b) The number of people in the household does not have a bearing on the premium charged.

c) Special damages are only awarded for small dollar amounts, such as out-of-pocket expenses.

d) Punitive damages are generally not covered by a liability policy.

8) Which of the following is not an example of a condition for liability claims?

a) The insured cannot voluntarily make any payment, assume any obligation, or incur expense other than necessary first aid expense.

b) Any documents received by the insured concerning the claim must be immediately sent to the insurer.

c) Bodily injury claims caused unintentionally by the insured may be covered.

d) Claims arising from the transmission of a communicable disease are covered.

9) Hyun owns a moped, a dirt bike, a motorized wheelchair, and a snowmobile. She asks which one is covered under Section II: Liability Coverages of the Comprehensive Homeowner Policy. What is your response?

a) The moped.

b) The dirt bike.

c) The motorized wheelchair.

d) The snowmobile.

10) An excess liability policy is available to:

a) Companies over $50M in gross sales.

b) Only those who are claim free.

c) Only those with primary insurance.

d) Cover additional limits and coverages.




CHAPTER 9

AUTOMOBILE INSURANCE

GOALS

By the end of this chapter, you will be able to:

✓ State the foundations of automobile insurance in Ontario.

✓ Identify the role of the Superintendent of Insurance and the Financial Services Regulatory Authority (FSRA).

✓ Identify the importance of the Take All Comers Rule.

✓ Summarize the mandatory coverages of the Ontario Automobile Policy: Owner’s Policy (OAP 1).

✓ Summarize the optional coverages of the OAP 1.

✓ Identify the key sections of the Ontario Application for Automobile Insurance: Owner’s Form (OAF 1).

✓ Explain the key sections of the OAP 1.

✓ Recall the importance of the automobile Statutory Conditions.

✓ Articulate the differences between the OAP 1, OAP 2 (Driver’s Policy), and OAP 4 (Garage Policy).

✓ Describe how fault determination rules function in the claims process.

✓ Reproduce the key endorsements for a personal lines automobile insurance policy.

9.1 INTRODUCTION

Automobile insurance in Ontario is a universal coverage that is both simple and complex.

It’s simple because the provincial government regulates what the drivers are required to have in their policies and the associated coverages. This creates consistency between all insurers’ products, and helps the consumer know what to expect at the time of a claim.

It’s complex because the provincial government included policy conditions to help clarify who is responsible at the time of a claim, but, in turn, also imposed strict guidelines in how insurers can administer their policies to adapt to a changing market.

One fact for any insurance broker is this: you will be dealing with a lot of automobile clients. How this policy was created — and how it links to government regulations — will be a necessary piece of knowledge when you discuss coverages with your clients.

This chapter consists of three parts:

1) Part 1: Background of Automobile Insurance.

2) Part 2: The Owner’s Policy (OAP 1).

3) Part 3: Facility Association, the Role of the Financial Services Regulatory Authority of Ontario, and Standard Policy Forms.

Throughout this chapter, we’ve included excerpts from the Ontario Automobile Policy: Owner’s Policy (OAP 1) wording to highlight key areas in the policy that you should know. We’ve also included a very important section immediately following Part 2, called Section 9.3.10: Ontario Policy Change Forms. This section lists and describes the major forms used to make changes, or endorsements, to the OAP 1. The OAF is not included with this textbook as it changes from time to time. To get the most current version of the OAF 1, go to this link and download the PDF form.

https://www.fsrao.ca/oaf-1-ontario-application-automobile-insurance-owners-form

This is a big chapter — so get comfortable, keep your OAP 1 and a notepad handy, and let’s begin!

9.2 PART 1: BACKGROUND OF AUTOMOBILE INSURANCE

This part of the chapter will cover:

• Background of Automobile Insurance.

• Different Insurers across Canada.

• Ontario Application for Automobile Insurance: Owner’s Form (OAF 1).

- Section 1: Name and Postal Address.

- Section 2: Policy Period.

- Section 3: Details of the Described Automobile.

- Section 4: Driver Information.

- Section 5: Previous Accidents and Insurance Claims.

- Section 6: History of Convictions.

- Section 7: Rating Information.

- Section 8: Insurance Coverages Applied For.

- Section 9: Remarks.

- Section 10: Method of Payment.

- Section 11: Declaration of Applicant.

- Section 12: Report of Broker/Agent.

• Approved Applications.

• Policy Renewals.

9.2.1 Introduction

Automobile accidents often result in injury, death, and loss to property. These losses amount to billions of dollars each year. Automobile owners need to protect themselves against the financial consequences of automobile accidents.

An automobile insurance policy can be purchased to provide coverage for:

• Third-party liability.

• Personal injury without regard to fault (accident benefits).

• Loss of or damage to the insured automobile.

9.2.2 Different Insurers across Canada

Automobile insurance is not the same in all provinces. In different provinces, automobile insurance is provided by private insurers, compulsory government insurance plans, and pure nofault and threshold no-fault insurance plans. We’ll look at the details of each of these insurers.

9.2.2.1 Private Insurers

Motorists in Alberta, Ontario, and the Atlantic provinces insure their automobiles with private insurers. In the majority of these provinces, coverage is provided by the Standard Policy Form (SPF) No. 1: Standard Automobile Policy (Owner’s Form), which was developed by the insurance industry for automobile owners. In the province of Ontario, the Ontario Automobile Policy: Owner’s Policy (OAP 1) is used to cover automobiles.

Insureds can use several standard endorsement forms (SEFs) to modify the coverages provided by this policy. In Ontario, the endorsements are available under the Ontario Policy Change Forms (OPCFs).

9.2.2.2 Compulsory Government Insurance Plans

The governments of British Columbia, Saskatchewan, and Manitoba have established compulsory government insurance plans for all motorists residing in those provinces. The coverages are different for each province, as shown in the graph below.
Province 	Third-party liability 	Accident benefits 	Physical damage
British Columbia 	$200,000 	Included 	Optional
Saskatchewan 	$200,000 	Included 	$500 deductible
Manitoba 	$200,000 	Included 	$400 deductible

All automobile owners are automatically entitled to purchase insurance equivalent to the minimum compulsory limits established by law. For this reason, provinces having compulsory government automobile insurance plans do not operate under the Facility Association. (Refer to Section 9.4.1 for more about the Facility Association.)

Private insurers compete with government insurance plans in these provinces for excess coverages only. These include:

• Increased third-party liability limits.

• Addition of physical damage coverages (British Columbia).

• Reduction of physical damage deductible (Saskatchewan and Manitoba).

When sold by private insurers, excess coverages are issued on a separate policy. Changes to these policies are generally made on the SEFs.

In Quebec, the government maintains a monopoly on personal injury coverages for accidents occurring in that province. Other coverages may be purchased from private insurers.

9.2.2.3 Pure No-Fault and Threshold No-Fault Insurance Plans

Some provinces have implemented “no-fault” automobile insurance plans. While these plans are not all the same, their primary purpose is to provide benefits to persons injured in automobile accidents, regardless of who is at fault.

A pure no-fault plan removes the right of legal action against drivers who cause injury or death to others. Instead, all accident victims (or their legal representatives) may claim compensation from their own insurers. This plan is used in Manitoba and Quebec.

A threshold no-fault plan is used in Ontario. Under this plan, legal action against the responsible party is not permitted unless a certain threshold is exceeded. The threshold requires two conditions that must be met for legal action against the negligent owner/driver (we’ll discuss these later in the chapter, in Section 9.3.6: Fault Determination Rules).

9.2.2.4 Facility Association

Insurance coverage must be available for every licensed driver. However, when an applicant fails to meet the underwriting standards established by the insurer (that is, if their record is not a risk an insurer wants to add to their book), coverage may be denied.
Has Facility Association 	Does Not Have Facility Association
Alberta 	British Columbia
New Brunswick 	Manitoba
Newfoundland & Labrador 	Saskatchewan
Northwest Territories 	Quebec
Nova Scotia 	
Nunavut 	
Ontario 	
Prince Edward Island 	
Yukon 	

Each of the provinces that participates in the Facility Association to ensure the availability of coverage for applicants who are refused insurance, also known as a pool. All insurers writing automobile liability insurance are members and share in the pooled results. When persons insured are unable to qualify for coverage in the marketplace, they are removed from the pool. We’ll learn more about the Facility Association in Section 9.4.1.

9.2.3 Ontario Application for Automobile Insurance: Owner’s Form (OAF 1)

The information necessary to properly underwrite, rate, and issue an automobile insurance policy is taken from a completed and signed application form. In Ontario, this form is the Ontario Application for Automobile Insurance: Owner’s Form (OAF 1).

To understand how the policy functions, let’s look at what it is asking the applicants. The insurer requires that all questions contained in the application be answered fully. More than one automobile can be insured by the same policy. Also, as this application is used to insure both private passenger and commercial automobiles, it contains questions specific to both uses. For commercially rated vehicles, the applicant must complete a separate commercial/public automobile supplement.

The OAF is not included with this textbook as it changes from time to time. To get the most current version of the OAF 1, go to this link and download the PDF form.

https://www.fsrao.ca/oaf-1-ontario-application-automobile-insurance-owners-form

9.2.3.1 Section 1: Name and Postal Address

The information in Section 1: Name and Postal Address is important for various reasons.

Before agreeing to provide the applicant with insurance, the insurer will request a driver history (also known as a “driver abstract”) from the provincial licensing body. Errors in providing the driver abstract are less likely to occur when the full name and address of the applicant are provided to the insurer.

The territory in which the automobile will be used has a bearing on the potential for loss. The insurer anticipates that the described automobile is in, and will be chiefly used in, the vicinity of the applicant’s address. If that assumption is incorrect, the applicant must provide all details. The application also requires that both the applicant’s residence and business telephone numbers be stated.

If the automobile being insured is leased, the lessor’s full name and postal address must be provided.

9.2.3.2 Section 2: Policy Period

The coverage begins at the time the application is taken and expires at 12:01 a.m. the local time at the address of the insured. This time and location is important because Ontario has different time zones. If an accident happens at 12:01 a.m. in Ottawa and another one happens simultaneously in Kenora, Kenora’s time of accident will be 11:01 p.m. This critical time difference means one automobile policy just expired, whereas the other has not.

Policies are normally issued for 12 months, meaning they need to be renewed yearly.

9.2.3.3 Section 3: Details of the Described Automobile

In the event of a claim, the insurer will want to be certain that the automobile for which coverage is being claimed is insured under the policy. When completing Section 3: Details of the Described Automobile, the applicant must provide the vehicle’s model, year, make or trade name, body type, cylinders or displacement, gross vehicle weight, and vehicle identification number (VIN). They must also disclose whether the vehicle is owned or leased, when it was purchased or leased, and the purchase price, including options and taxes. Other questions pertain to use of the vehicle: commuting distance to work (one way), annual driving distances, details of any carpooling, type of fuel (gas or diesel), and disclosure of any unrepaired damages. Any modifications — such as non-factory wheels, electronic equipment, or fancy paint alterations — must be added to the Remarks section. In the OAF 1, page 2, Special Note 1 specifies a $1,500 limit on after-factory installed electronic accessories and equipment in the vehicle.

The applicant must also provide the name and address of any lienholder and a declaration as to the applicant being the registered owner and the actual owner. A copy of the vehicle registration identifies the owner of the vehicle, and the policy must be in the name registered with the Ministry of Transportation.

The policy contains exclusions for certain uses of the vehicle. The applicant must indicate if the vehicle is being rented or leased, carrying passengers for compensation or hire, hauling a trailer, or carrying explosives or radioactive materials. If the answer to any of these questions is “yes,” the applicant may require additional coverage by endorsement.

The final question asks for the total number of automobiles in the household. This includes vehicles belonging to other household members (for example, vehicles belonging to the applicant’s children or company-supplied vehicles). Note that if any of these vehicles are already insured on another policy, they would not be included in this application. This question allows the underwriter to check the history of these drivers; if their history is found to be not satisfactory, the underwriter may want to exclude them from the applicant’s policy.

This information is also important for rating purposes when physical damage coverages are required. Insurers maintain information on the costs to repair or replace various makes and models of automobiles. Generally, repair costs increase as the value of the automobile increases. This means that insureds having expensive automobiles will pay a higher premium for physical damage coverages than owners of lesser-value automobiles. Some insurers also charge more for insuring certain types of highpowered or customized cars.

In a recent trend, automobile manufacturers have added more sophisticated equipment into previously hollow parts, like bumpers or side mirrors. Now, they have cameras, proximity sensors, or accelerometers, making these once cheap replacement parts much more expensive. Sometimes it’s not just the brand name that makes a vehicle expensive — it’s the parts that go into it.

To assist the insurer in the settlement of future claims, the applicant is also required to provide the following information:

• If leased by the applicant: name and address of the lessor.

• If purchased by the applicant: new or used, date, and value (including equipment).

• Name and address of any lienholder or mortgagee.

When others have a financial interest in the automobile being insured (such as a mortgagee), their interest is recorded and protected in the event of a loss.

9.2.3.4 Section 4: Driver Information

Automobile insurance policies permit the use of the automobile by persons not named on the policy. Section 4: Driver Information is designed to identify anyone who will regularly or occasionally use the insured automobile.

The applicant is required to provide the following information:

• Names of all drivers (as shown on their driver’s licenses).

• Approximate percentage of their use of the automobile.

• Number of years licensed.

If drivers have graduated from a driver training course, a copy of that certificate should be included with the application. If there are other licensed drivers in the household, they must also be listed in the Remarks section.

The OAF 1 also asks whether any of the drivers qualify for a retiree discount. Special Note 2: Retiree Discount explains the qualification requirements to obtain this discount. The discount applies if the principal operator:

• Is retired.

• Is age 65 or older or receiving a pension.

• Has no earned income.

• Is not working.

• Has not been employed for the last 26 weeks in the last 52 weeks.

• Is not operating a business.

It also indicates that, if the applicant qualifies, they must sign a declaration that they are a retiree.

Near the end of this section, the applicant must provide information about their most recent insurer, including the policy number and expiry date.

Further questions ask whether the applicant knows of:

• Any suspensions or cancellations of the driver’s licenses of any listed driver within the last six years.

• Any cancellation of insurance within the last three years for the applicant or any listed driver.

• Any claim denied for material misrepresentation within the past three years for the applicant or any listed driver.

• Any fraud connected with automobile insurance by the applicant or any listed driver. (There is no time restraint on this question.)

If the answer to any of the above questions is “yes”, the applicant must provide details in the Remarks section.

9.2.3.5 Section 5: Previous Accidents and Insurance Claims

In Section 5: Previous Accidents and Insurance Claims, the applicant must provide details of any and all previous accidents and claims for the last six years. This information applies to all owned vehicles, or use or operation of any automobile, including where they were listed as a driver. Any accidents that occurred while the applicant was driving a non-owned automobile (such as an employer’s) must also be disclosed.

The previous accident and claim information must include the following, to the best of the applicant’s knowledge: drivers, vehicles, dates, types of claim, and amounts paid.

9.2.3.6 Section 6: History of Convictions

The insurer will carefully screen all applicants to ensure that insurance is not provided to anyone who has an undesirable driver history. They will check the applicant’s convictions history from the previous three years, including offences for impaired driving, speeding, and failure to yield the right of way. Even though offences may not have resulted in an accident, they may point to poor driving habits or a disregard for the law.

Section 6: History of Convictions requires the date and description of each conviction.

9.2.3.7 Section 7: Rating Information

Section 7: Rating Information is for the insurance broker or company to develop the rating information based on all the previous sections of the application. It will indicate:

• The driving class.

• Driving record for each section of the policy.

• Claim or conviction surcharges.

• Vehicle price or code.

• Applicable rate groups for accident benefits.

• Direct compensation and physical damage.

• Location and territory.

• Any discounts that may apply.

This section will determine the premium to be charged for the coverages selected in Section 8: Insurance Coverages Applied For.

9.2.3.8 Section 8: Insurance Coverages Applied For

Section 8: Insurance Coverages Applied For allows the applicant to select the coverages required, including any optional increased accident benefits or endorsements. They may also select limits and deductibles.

A total premium is shown per vehicle; however, the applicant needs to be informed that the policy premium shown is an estimated premium and subject to adjustment or confirmation by the insurer. The applicant also needs to be informed that there will be a minimum premium charge if the applicant cancels their policy.

9.2.3.9 Section 9: Remarks

Section 9: Remarks allows the applicant to provide further details for any of the above application sections.

9.2.3.10 Section 10: Method of Payment

Section 10: Method of Payment identifies the type of payment plan, estimated policy premium, tax, interest, and total estimated cost. Separate boxes are for amount paid, balance owing, number of remaining installments, amount of each installment, and due date.

9.2.3.11 Section 11: Declaration of Applicant

Section 11: Declaration of Applicant is where the applicant signs to attest that they understand the purpose and factual accuracy of the information they have provided, including that the drivers:

• Must not suffer from mental or health-related disabilities that will interfere with the ability to drive safely.

• Must not be addicted to alcohol or drugs to the extent that it will interfere with their ability to drive safely.

• Must notify the Ministry of Transportation if they become physically or emotionally disabled to the extent that it will interfere with their ability to drive safely.

The applicant warrants that all drivers are qualified to hold a driver’s license and that all completed sections of the application are correct. Warning is given for false statements, misrepresentations, and fraud.

The Insurance Act requires that the insured be given clear warning of the consequences for making a false statement on the application for insurance and any subsequent claim brought under the policy. Generally, the right to recover under the policy is forfeited when the insured:

• Provides a false description of the automobile being insured which is to the prejudice of the insurer. For example, manufacturers normally make two or more models of the same automobile. When the difference between them is substantial, the premium required to insure them may vary significantly. When an applicant seeking a lower premium describes the automobile as being of a lesser model than it actually is, such description prejudices the position of the insurer.

• Knowingly misrepresents the risk or fails to disclose a required fact. Misrepresentation can often be traced to the applicant’s statements regarding previous accidents, refusals of insurance, or the intended use of the vehicle.

• Breaches a policy term. The most common contraventions are usually of those set out in the Statutory Conditions.

• Commits a fraud. Fraud is committed, for example, when insureds attempt to claim for loss or damage to the automobile which was deliberately caused by them.

• Willfully makes a false statement in respect of a claim. The word “willfully” suggests that the insured intends to deceive the insurer into paying for a claim which is not insured by the policy. False statements typically concern the consumption of alcohol, the amount of the loss, the identity of the driver, and consent to use the automobile.

The applicant warrants the truth of all statements made in the application for insurance and further agrees to permit the insurer to obtain, from any source, reports containing personal, credit, factual, investigative, or driver record information in connection with the application, renewal, extension, or variation thereof.

When insurers need to verify the truth of the information contained in the application, they rely on two common sources: (1) Ontario’s Ministry of Transportation or such other provincial registry having jurisdiction and (2) the applicant’s previous insurer, the name of which must be disclosed in the application, if applicable.

9.2.3.12 Section 12: Report of Broker/Agent

Section 12: Report of Broker/Agent is completed by the broker/agent and indicates:

• If the coverage has been bound.

• If the business is new to the broker.

• The type of liability card issued (for example, temporary or permanent).

• If the applicant is known to the broker.

• How long the broker has known the principal driver.

9.2.4 Take All Comers Rule

As we have read earlier, the Insurance Act in Ontario makes automobile insurance mandatory in the province for any driver on the road. As such, there has also been some consumer protection built onto the legislation, ensuring that all consumers are able receive a quotation for automobile insurance, at a fair price according to the rates and regulations filed by auto insurers with FSRA, to help enforce this law. The Ontario law prohibits insurers from denying a basic auto policy to anyone, except on grounds filed with the regulator. This law is known as the “take all comers rule” (TAC). Any insurer caught failing to comply with this legislation can face a varying range and degree of punishments from FSRA.

For brokers, however, it is their responsibility to provide the best possible coverage at the lowest possible price that meets the client’s needs. To do that, a broker must go to all available insurers their brokerage has to seek auto quotes for their customers. If an insurer fails to provide a quotation on an application for a client, it is then up to the broker to inform the customer of this act of the insurer, and also to document the situation accordingly. Whenever this situation is encountered, it is important for a broker to have thorough documentation and be able to explain the situation with their customer.

Let’s look at two brief examples;

Jarrod has had three claims in one year, and his policy is up for renewal. All of the claims were not-at-fault claims. Jarrod’s insurance company, however, responds to the renewal that may be a higher risk for future claims and refuses to offer a renewal on that reason. However, the insurer did not file that specific underwriting rule with FSRA. The insurer is therefore in violation of the TAC rule.

Myriam, a broker, enters a prospect’s auto application into the brokerage’s quoting system, but only four of their six markets provided her a quote. The client has a clean driving history. Recognizing the issue here, Myriam decides to verify why those two markets did not quote and also checks where she can try to obtain a quote from in another way. Myriam took detailed notes. Only after doing this work did Myriam contact her prospect and present all options to them, with explanations.

9.2.5 Approved Applications

The Insurance Act states that the submitted application must form an actual part of the whole contract. OAP 1, Section 1.1 states that

“The contract includes three documents:

• A completed and signed Application for Automobile Insurance,

• A Certificate of Automobile Insurance, and

• This policy.”

When the application is acceptable to the insurer, the underwriter will issue the policy. For this reason, it is essential that all questions be answered fully and honestly.

Upon approval, the insurer will issue a Certificate of Automobile Insurance to the applicant. The certificate will include the information from the application, as well as the limits, deductibles, term of coverage, endorsements, and premium.

The front of the application has a brief explanation of the coverage sections. However, the coverage may not be the same as what was applied for, because the coverage is subject to decisions made by the insurer at the underwriting stage. Some common differences may be higher deductibles or additional restricting endorsements.

The certificate will indicate that the client can obtain a copy of the complete OAP 1 policy if requested. However, very few requests for complete policy wordings are ever made, so the Certificate of Automobile Insurance is the confirmation of coverage to the client.

9.2.6 Policy Renewals

When a policy is renewed, the insurer will issue a new Certificate of Automobile Insurance which indicates the new inception and expiration dates. If the insurer changes any other terms, the policy is deemed to be offered on a varied terms basis.

Additionally, if there are varied terms (for example, deductible limits are higher), the Ontario Insurance Act requires that the insurer give notice to the broker not less than 45 days prior to the renewal of such varied terms. The broker must similarly give notice to the client not less than 30 days before the renewal date. If the broker does not give the 30 days’ notice, the broker could be held responsible for the difference in the varied terms. In order to prevent errors and omissions (E&O) claims against the broker and/or the brokerage, brokers need to advise clients immediately when an insurer wishes to renew a policy on varied terms.

If an insurer does not wish to offer a renewal, this will also constitute a varied term. In these situations, the broker must either (1) replace the policy with an alternate insurer or (2) advise the client of the insurer’s position at least 30 days before the renewal date. Failing to advise the client within this time could make the broker responsible for the claim.

Effective since 1990, the Certificate of Automobile Insurance must disclose to the client how their policy is rated. However, it is not good enough to show a rate class 02 or 03 because this has little meaning to the general public — instead, the certificate must show an explanation of the rate class in plain language. This allows the insured to make an intelligent comparison of coverages and the criteria. This rate class disclosure includes chargeable claims, discounts that may be available, class description (02, 03, 07, etc.), driving record description, and surcharges.

9.3 PART 2: THE OWNER’S POLICY (OAP 1)

This part of the chapter will cover:

• Introduction to the OAP 1.

• Plain Language Wordings.

• Mandatory Coverages.

• Optional Coverages.

• Notice of Accident.

• Fault Determination Rules.

• Motor Vehicle Liability Insurance Card.

• Sections of the OAP 1:

- Section 1: Introduction.

- Section 2: What Automobiles Are Covered.

- Section 3: Liability Coverage.

- Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage.

- Section 5: Uninsured Automobile Coverage.

- Section 6: Direct Compensation – Property Damage Coverage.

- Section 7: Loss or Damage Coverages (Optional).

- Section 8: Statutory Conditions.

• Ontario Policy Change Forms.

9.3.1 Introduction to the OAP 1

In this section, we’ll examine all the component parts of the Ontario Automobile Policy: Owner’s Policy (OAP 1). Learning this content will help you during your exam (because knowing which sections contain specific information will help you quickly find the answers) and help you explain to your clients how their policies will work for them. Although the OAP 1 refers to coverages and amounts, it does not contain the schedule of those amounts, because the schedule is adjusted from time to time. You can find a summary of this schedule on IBAO’s website on Ontario Auto for reference,

https://www.ibao.org/materials/ontario-auto/

9.3.2 Plain Language Wordings

Ontario was the first province to approve a plain language automobile policy. “Plain language” means that the legal and insurance jargon and technical conditions found in old policies would be “translated” so that the general public could understand them.

The OAP 1 came into effect March 31, 1994. The policy has changed over time, but the most current version was approved for use on September 1, 2010 by the Financial Services Commission of Ontario (the predecessor to the Financial Services Regulatory Authority of Ontario).

Using plain language wording helps average Ontario residents to easily understand their coverage on their own. In addition to the then-new policy format, new endorsement forms in Ontario were also written in plain language.

9.3.3 Mandatory Coverages

To ensure that no one being insured to drive in Ontario is left without necessary coverages, the Ontario government established a set of directives for the automobile insurance industry to bring in standardized, or mandatory, coverages.

On November 1, 1996, the Automobile Insurance Rate Stability Act, 1996 became law. This act set the minimum standard for all automobile insurance policy coverages in Ontario. One of the government’s main goals was to deter people from intentionally driving without insurance; as a result, the fine for this offence was increased to $5,000, up to a maximum of $25,000 for the first offence, and from $10,000 to a maximum of $50,000 for the second offence.

The act also set other basic ground rules and mandatory coverages for all licensed drivers in Ontario. Here’s an overview of these mandatory coverages — we’ll explore them in much greater detail later in the chapter.

9.3.3.1 Section 3: Liability Coverage

Since March 1, 1981, the minimum liability coverage in Section 3: Liability Coverage has been set to $200,000 for property damage and bodily injury, combined. If a claim reaches or exceeds this limit, then injury claims take priority, and the amount is apportioned as follows:

• $190,000 for bodily injury.

• $10,000 for property damage.

9.3.3.2 Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage

The accident benefits under Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage are often referred to as “nofault benefits” and are set out in the Statutory Accident Benefits Schedule (SABS). (The SABS can be found in Regulation 403/96 of the Insurance Act.) Although these are mandatory, the insured also has the option of purchasing additional accident benefits to supplement the basic mandatory benefits. The SABS includes:

• Income replacement benefit.

• Non-earner benefit.

• Caregiver benefit.

• Dependant care benefit.

• Medical, rehabilitation, and attendant care benefits.

• Payment of other expenses.

• Death and funeral benefits.

• Optional benefits (for example, increased limits for the above benefits, indexation of benefits payable, etc.).

9.3.3.3 Section 5: Uninsured Automobile Coverage

Section 5: Uninsured Automobile Coverage provides protection for the insured, their spouses, and their children, as well as the operators of the described automobile, if they are struck and injured by an uninsured automobile.

9.3.3.4 Section 6: Direct Compensation — Property Damage Coverage

Section 6: Direct Compensation — Property Damage Coverage, known as DCPD, responds when there is damage to the insured’s automobile, its contents, or its loss of use due to an accident in Ontario that is not the insured’s fault.

On January 1, 2024, the Ontario Government made its first change to the OAP 1 by creating an opt-out option for consumers from the DCPD mandatory coverage. Known as OPCF 49: Agreement Not to Recover for Loss or Damage from an Automobile Collision, this endorsement permits the policyholder to not have DCPD on their policy if they are the legal owner of the vehicle (i.e. in other words, they are not leasing the vehicle). This change form also makes cascading changes to Section 7: Optional Coverages, specifically to collision and the comprehensive coverages, as those sections rely on DCPD being in place.

9.3.4 Optional Coverages

When we describe some coverages as “optional,” we do not mean they are “add-ons” or less necessary than the mandatory coverages — rather, these coverages extend the mandatory coverages into key areas that drivers will benefit from.

Optional coverages include the following:

• Increased liability limits: The minimum limits in Ontario are $200,000, but most policies are issued with higher limits, such as $1,000,000 or higher.

• Physical damage (loss or damage) coverages: These coverages are designed to indemnify policyholders for physical damage to their automobile. They may be called “all perils,” “collision,” “comprehensive,” or “specified perils” coverage. Each of them covers damage to the vehicle itself, as well as coverage for damage caused by different perils. A deductible (in variable amounts) always applies, unless the loss is caused by fire or lightning.

• Optional accident benefits coverages: Insureds can increase the coverage limits specified in the SABS to allow for better than standard coverage. We’ll discuss these limits, as well as the optional increases, in more detail in Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverages.

• Other coverage and/or policy changes: Optional coverage is provided through endorsements known as the Ontario Policy Change Forms (OPCFs). These forms are used to indicate changes to the basic provisions of the policy. Later in the chapter, we’ll look at the most common endorsements (Section 9.3.10: Ontario Policy Change Forms).

9.3.5 Notice of Accident

An insured person is required to report an accident to their automobile insurer within seven days of an accident if:

• The accident has been reported to the police under the Highway Traffic Act (for example, for injury, criminal acts, damage over $2000, etc.); or

• The accident will be reported to an agent or broker, regardless of who is at fault.

If the insured is unable to report the accident due to incapacity, they may comply with this condition by reporting the accident as soon as they regain the ability to do so. If a third party has a right of recovery against the policy, then that party may make the claim on their own behalf, as stated in the Statutory Conditions.

9.3.6 Fault Determination Rules

The Fault Determination Rules (which can be found in the Insurance Act, Regulation 668) contain examples of common types of collisions. The rules also describe how fault is assigned for insurance purposes — for example, by using the Fault Chart. The Fault Chart covers the most common types of accidents and includes diagrams illustrating the points of impact between two or more cars. The diagrams illustrate how to apportion fault between the vehicles. The Fault Chart rules are based on the rules of the road and tort law precedents set in past cases.

Let’s look at these examples.

Image

If an insured disagrees with the liability derived from the Fault Determination Rules, they have the option of taking legal action against the insurer. If an insured disagrees with the amount of a settlement offered for damage to the vehicle, they can refer to OAP 1, Section 6.7.3: Settling a Claim and ask for an appraisal. In these situations, the insured and the insurer will each appoint an appraiser who will either agree on an amount or will appoint an umpire to decide between their respective positions.

9.3.7 Motor Vehicle Liability Insurance Card

Like other Canadian jurisdictions, Ontario requires proof of financial responsibility. As of March 1994, the Motor Vehicle Liability Insurance Card, commonly referred to as the “pink slip,” was introduced to Ontarians. This certificate clearly indicates coverage is provided within Canada and the United States.

9.3.8 Sections of the OAP 1

We’ll now take an in-depth look at the most important parts of the OAP 1. It’s important to learn the OAP 1 in its entirety. However, we’ll expand on some specific sections in the pages that follow. You’ll want to keep your copy handy as you review these sections!

9.3.8.1 Section 1: Introduction

Section 1: Introduction provides definitions of terms and explains information that applies to the entire policy. For example, it includes:

• Where the insured is covered.

• How to cancel the policy.

• Who and what the insurance company will not cover.

• Where to make a claim and who may make it.

9.3.8.1.1 Section 1.1: This Policy is Part of a Contract

“This policy is part of a contract between you and us. The contract includes three documents:

• A completed and signed Application for Automobile Insurance,

• A Certificate of Automobile Insurance, and

• This policy.”

9.3.8.1.2 Section 1.2: Where You Are Covered (Territory)

“This policy covers you and other insured persons for incidents occurring in Canada, the United States of America and any other jurisdiction designated in the Statutory Accident Benefits Schedule, and on a vessel travelling between ports of those countries.

All of the dollar limits described in this policy are in Canadian funds.”

9.3.8.1.3 Section 1.3: Definitions

Under the OAP 1, the definition of an automobile includes the “described automobile” — that is, the automobile insured under the contract and described by its year, make, model, and serial number within the policy. But it can also mean a “newly acquired automobile,” “temporary substitute automobile,” or an all-encompassing “other automobile.” Each automobile defined is covered by the policy to varying degrees. The definition of an automobile under the OAP 1 also includes motorized snow vehicles, such as snowmobiles.

The policy defines an “excluded driver” as someone specifically not covered by the policy. The names of excluded drivers are always endorsed onto the policy using OPCF 28A: Excluded Driver Endorsement. This endorsement restricts all coverages (other than certain accident benefits coverages) if the excluded driver drives the described, newly acquired, temporary substitute, or other automobile as defined in the policy.

Note that the definition of “insured person” can change from one section to another. For example, in Section 1.3: Definitions, “named insured” is defined as “the person or organization to whom the Certificate of Automobile Insurance is issued.” However, persons covered under Section 3: Liability Coverage are “you [named insured], or anyone else in possession of the automobile with your consent, [who] uses or operates it.” Meanwhile, under Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage, insured persons “are defined in the Statutory Accident Benefits Schedule. In addition, insured persons also include any person who is injured or killed in an automobile accident involving the automobile and is not the named insured, or the spouse or dependant of a named insured, under any other motor vehicle liability policy, and is not covered under the policy of an automobile in which they were an occupant or which struck them.”

9.3.8.1.4 Section 1.4: Your Responsibilities

The policy states that, by accepting the OAP 1 contract, the insured agrees to the conditions listed in this section. It also states that if the insured fails to meet any of the listed conditions, then claims under the policy will be denied (with the exception of certain accident benefits). Let’s look at some of the key responsibilities.

9.3.8.1.4.1 Section 1.4.1

“You agree to notify us promptly and in writing of any significant change of which you are aware in your status as a driver, owner or lessee of a described automobile. [ . . . ] You must promptly tell us of any change in information supplied in your original application for insurance, such as additional drivers, or a change in the way a described automobile is used.”

This condition is derived from Section 8: Statutory Conditions of the policy. It reinforces the importance of keeping the details of the insurance contract accurate and up to date. Even small details — like the distance the vehicle is used to commute to work — need to be updated if, for example, the insured’s job changes.

All questions asked on an application are used for rating the policy; that is, they are all factors used in determining the premium to be charged. A short, two-kilometre drive to work will usually result in a lower premium than a 24-kilometre drive to work.

9.3.8.1.4.2 Section 1.4.5

“You agree not to drive or operate the automobile or allow anyone else to drive or operate the automobile, when not authorized by law.”

Conditions 1.4.5 and 1.4.6 are very important conditions within the contract. Collectively, they maintain coverage under the contract only when the vehicle is being used lawfully. Examples of people not authorized by law to drive include unlicensed drivers, those whose license is under suspension, and minors.

9.3.8.1.4.3 Section 1.4.6

“You agree not to use or allow anyone to use the automobile in a race or speed test or for any illegal trade or transportation.”

Using the vehicle in a race or speed test places both the vehicle and the passengers at greater risk of an accident than would normally be expected. This type of increased risk is not considered when calculating the premium for the policy.

As mentioned, the policy states that if the insured fails to meet any of the responsibilities in this section, claims under the policy (with the exception of certain accident benefits) will be denied. Correspondingly, this means that certain accident benefits will still be payable regardless of any breach of these conditions. For example, an excluded driver operating the vehicle will still qualify for the medical, rehabilitation, and attendant care benefits if they are injured in an accident. (We’ll cover accident benefits in detail later in this chapter, in Section 9.3.8.4: Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage.)

9.3.8.1.5 Section 1.6: Our Rights and Responsibilities

The three subsections that follow are each, in some way, related to the settlement of any dispute over money. Claims, premium payments, and premium over- and underpayments are all addressed here.

9.3.8.1.5.1 Section 1.6.1: Payment of Claims

“We will pay legitimate claims within 60 days of receiving a proof of loss. Some claims for Accident Benefits will be paid sooner. If we refuse to pay a claim, we will notify the insured person in writing explaining the reasons why we are not liable to pay.”

The policy refers here to a proof of loss. A proof of loss is a formal statement made by the insured to the insurer regarding a claim, giving details of the loss so the insurer can determine the extent of its liability.

After an accident, an insurance company will usually send the insured a proof of loss form to complete. It is essentially a blank piece of paper, and the insured is required to write an explanation of the events which led up to the accident, such as:

• The parties involved.

• Any witnesses who were present.

• The degree of damages.

• The amount claimed and any other insurable interest of others or incumbrances.

• All other pertinent details about the loss and the resulting claim.

Note that the policy states that legitimate claims will be paid within 60 days of the insurer receiving the proof of loss — not 60 days from the date of the accident.

Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage is designed to help the insured pay for any medical services they require. Since medical attention is often required immediately following an accident, certain components of Section 4: Accident Benefits Coverage will be paid sooner than the prescribed 60 days.

9.3.8.1.5.2 Section 1.6.2: If You Have Been Incorrectly Classified and Your Premium is Wrong

“If the incorrect classification resulted in your paying too high a premium, we will refund any premium overpayment with interest.

[ . . . ]

If the incorrect classification resulted in your paying too low a premium, we will require you to pay an additional premium as long as we tell you within 60 days of the effective date of the policy. We will not charge you interest on the additional premium.”

Incorrect classifications occasionally happen. If an application is incorrectly read by an underwriter and a policy is issued based upon the error, the premium charged will most likely be incorrect. An error of this sort will not go against the insured. This section addresses both overpayment and underpayment situations. Notice the 60-day reclassification window available to the insurer.

If the insurer does not notice the underpayment within this time period, it must honour the premium quoted until the renewal of the policy. However, an overpayment by the insured is not subject to any such timeline. Overpayment, to the knowledge of the insurer, must always be refunded, regardless of when it occurred.

Note: An incorrect classification due to the omission or the misrepresentation of a material fact on an application is not subject to the same 60-day rule. If an applicant knowingly misrepresents facts to the prejudice of the insurer, the insurer may reclassify the risk at any point upon discovering the misrepresentation.

9.3.8.1.5.3 Section 1.6.3: Monthly Premium Payment Option

Section 1.6.3: Monthly Premium Payment Option allows the insurer to add an interest charge to the premium payable. This is only if the insured chooses to pay the premium over a 12-month period instead of a total payment upfront for the coverage period.

9.3.8.1.6 Section 1.7: Cancelling Your Insurance

As we learned earlier, the OAP 1 is written in plain language, and Section 1.7: Cancelling Your Insurance is a plain language restatement of Statutory Condition 11: Termination. This section explains the differences between an insurer’s cancellation and an insured’s cancellation of the policy.

Section 1.7.1: When You Cancel explains what happens when an insured cancels the policy. An insured can cancel their policy at any time. A refund, if any, is generated by calculating the portion of premium collected which is so far unused and subtracting an administrative charge from that amount. This is commonly referred to as a “short rate” refund. The unearned premium is returned to the insured, less the deducted fee.

Section 1.7.2: When We Cancel explains what happens when an insurer cancels the policy. An insurer can cancel the policy for any of the reasons listed in the wording under this section. This section mentions “reason[s] [ . . . ] filed with the Financial Services Regulatory Authority”; this refers to a process where every insurer files their own cancellation reasons with the FSRA. Every insurance company designs its own guidelines for risks it will accept and risks it deems to be outside its target market segment. Among other things, these rules include how many accidents and/or driving convictions (speeding tickets, etc.) a person can have before the company considers the risk too high to accept. These guidelines are then submitted to the FSRA for approval. If the FSRA decides the submissions are fair, the company is allowed to use the guidelines as its “declination rules” — that is, their reasons to decline a risk. If a policy is issued by a broker and it is later discovered that a driver listed on the automobile policy has more convictions than the company’s filed regulations allow, the company can cancel the policy within 60 days of its issuance. Any refund issued after an insurer has cancelled the policy will consist of the complete unearned premium collected (no administration charge is deducted). This is known as a “pro rata” or “proportionate basis” refund.

If there is a minimum retained premium stated in the Certificate of Automobile Insurance, that amount will not be refunded in either the short rate or the pro rata cancellation process.

Section 1.7.3: How We Can Cancel for Non-payment of Premium refers to the first two strikes of the so-called “three strikes” cancellation for non-payment process. If an insured is cancelled for nonpayment of premiums, the notification of cancellation must provide:

• 30 days’ notice, if sent by registered mail.

• 10 days’ notice, if hand delivered.

This notice must state the amount owed to the insurer at the time the notice is sent. The mailing or delivering of this notification meets the legal requirement for the insurer to provide “notice” — however, the 30- or 10-day notification period begins on the second day after the notice has been mailed (i.e. if the notice was mailed on a Monday, the notice begins on the Wednesday). The insured now has the option of allowing the policy to cancel on the stated date, or pay the amount stated on the notification which is owed to the insurer before noon of the business day prior to the stated cancellation date. If the insured pays the owed amount by that time, the cancellation procedure is stopped, and the policy remains in force as though no cancellation notice had been sent.

If the insured misses another payment and the insurer initiates the cancellation procedure for the second time, the same conditions would apply.

However, upon the third cancellation for non-payment, the cancellation notice becomes the same as a cancellation for any other reason, as explained in Section 1.7.4: How We Can Cancel for Repeated Non-payment or Other Reasons. The required notice period is 15 days from the day following the day of the notification mailing, or five days if personally hand delivered. The insurer is under no obligation to reinstate the policy — even if the amount owing is paid before the cancellation date. The two previous notification periods would have stated the amount owed at the time the notice was mailed or personally delivered; however, on the third notice, the amount stated is the amount owed to insure the policyholder until the stipulated cancellation date.

It is important to note the cancellation procedure resets itself upon each policy renewal. There must be three non-payments in a policy term before the cancellation can be delivered as described in Section 1.7.4: How We Can Cancel for Repeated Non-payment or Other Reasons.

9.3.8.1.6.1 Section 1.7.1: When You Cancel

“You may cancel your insurance at any time by advising us.

If you cancel, we will calculate the premium you owe on a short rate basis. Short rate means that the premium you owe will include our handling costs. We will refund anything due to you as soon as possible.

There may be a minimum premium set out in your Certificate of Automobile Insurance. This will not be refunded.”

9.3.8.1.6.2 Section 1.7.2: When We Cancel

“Where your policy has been in effect for less than 60 days, we may only cancel your policy for a reason that we have filed with Financial Services Regulatory Authority. Where your policy has been in effect for more than 60 days, we may only cancel your policy for one of the following reasons:

• Non-payment of premium,

• You have given false particulars of the automobile to our prejudice,

• You have knowingly misrepresented or failed to disclose information that you were required to provide in the application for automobile insurance, or

• The risk has changed materially.

If we cancel your policy, we will calculate the premium you owe on a proportionate basis. Proportionate means you will pay for the actual number of days you were covered. For example, if half the premium period is over, you will pay half the premium.

There may be a minimum premium shown on your Certificate of Automobile Insurance. This will not be refunded.

If you have paid more than the premium you owe, we will refund the difference on cancellation. Your refund may be delayed if the amount of premium you owe is subject to adjustment, or we are waiting for reports in order to determine the premium paid or owing. We will make the refund as soon as possible in that case.”

9.3.8.1.6.3 Section 1.7.3: How We Can Cancel for Non-Payment of Premium

“In case of non-payment of premium, we may give you a notice in writing. We must give you ten days notice if we deliver the notice in person, or 30 days notice by sending the notice by registered mail to your last known address. The 30-day period starts on the second day after we mail the registered letter. The notice will inform you that you have until noon of the business day before the last day of the notice period to pay the arrears, plus an administration fee, failing which the policy will automatically be cancelled effective at 12:01 a.m. on the last day of the notice period. If you pay the arrears and the administration fee in time, then your policy will not be cancelled.

But if we have already given you two notices of non-payment of premium during the term of your policy and a non-payment occurs again, we don’t have to give you another notice under this section; instead we may cancel your policy as described in section 1.7.4.”

9.3.8.1.6.4 Section 1.7.4: How We Can Cancel for Repeated Non-Payment or Other Reasons

“If we cancel your insurance for non-payment of premium because we have already given you two notices during the term of your policy as described in section 1.7.3, or if we cancel for any other reason, we will notify you in writing. We must give you five days notice if we deliver the notice of cancellation in person, or 15 days notice by sending the notice of cancellation by registered mail to your last known address. The 15-day period starts on the second day after we mail the registered letter. If the notice was given because we have already given you two notices of non-payment during the term of your policy as described in section 1.7.3, we are under no obligation to accept a late payment or to keep the policy in force after the effective date of cancellation.”

9.3.8.1.7 SECTION 1.8: WHO AND WHAT WE WON’T COVER

9.3.8.1.7.1 Section 1.8.1: General Exclusion

Section 1.8.1: General Exclusion states that the vehicle is not to be used to carry explosives or radioactive material, nor will it be used to carry paying passengers (that is, it won’t be used as a taxi or a bus). The definition of “paying passengers” does not include carpools, friends sharing costs, carrying domestic workers, carrying children for activities related to educational programs, carrying elderly or special needs passengers while volunteering within the community, or carrying current or prospective clients and/or customers.

The wording in this section begins with the phrase: “Except for certain Accident Benefits coverage, there is no coverage under this policy if…”. There are two very important points within this phrase. Sections 1.8.1: General Exclusion to 1.8.5: Losses Due to War Activities Not Covered list uses and drivers of the automobile that the policy does not cover.

Neither the driver’s potential legal liability, nor the potential damage to the vehicle itself, will be covered if any of these uses occur or if any of these excluded drivers operate the vehicle. These exclusions represent risks that the OAP 1 was never meant to insure. Coverage can be purchased to allow these activities, either through (1) endorsement and an additional premium or (2) a different insurance policy altogether.
